B000002UB3|5.0|I wasn't going to do a review of a Beatles album for some time but as it is Beatles Day here in England then I can hardly pay a small tribute to the fab four.    I cannot say anything new about Abbey Road, it has had nearly four decades of reviews and praise. Just think about it, this work of art is nearly fourty years old. Compare it to the `best' albums of last year. It still feels fresh and original and outranks anything that last year had to offer.    This was the last album recorded by the Beatles, after a stunning career of sublime hits and wondrous works of art they top it all off by one of their best efforts and one of the best albums ever. My favourite has always been Revolver but Abbey Road has always been second place only just. These days I think this album my just be my favourite Beatles Album.    This is such an iconic work of the twentieth century, from the famous and often imitated cover, to the fabulous melodies and lyrics. Which brings me to ask: Why are you reading this? If it is because you do not own this album, or even worse, have never heard it then quickly get it ordered and downloaded before someone sees you. Everyone should own this, in fact, in England you are sent it in the post once you become of age. It is that important to everyday life. That may be over the top but it certainly is essential to everyone's music collection, even if you only have a few albums.    One of the joys is that each member of the band seems to be at their best on this album. Even Ringo proves his critics wrong by showing them up with a great drum solo during the album's closing medley. Paul belts out some cracking vocals, very powerful stuff, John rocks like never before and George presents some of his finest songs ever. Individually they all shine in their own right and when they come together (pardon the pun) they shake the earth with their brilliance.    This album has the traits of genius running all the way through it from beginning to end. It is the traitthat binds every song together to deliver the world a package of true beauty that continues to inspire and move people even after all these years.    There are a few weak tracks, mainly novelty songs, but these are merely small blips on an otherwise shining record. The highs are certainly well worth the lows.    Buy it now. Listen to it forever. You will never tire of it and neither will your neighbours if you turn it up.    Come Together- Paul's bass line opens the album, then kick in the crazy lyrics. This is a good track but I wouldn't place it in say their top 10 songs. It is just missing something to take it beyond the norm.....for a Beatles track anyway, which means it's still pretty good.    Something- A simple beautiful song, George Harrison really knows how to write a moving song whilst keeping it simple, even the brilliant guitar solo is just a simple and wonderful piece of music. Absolutely nothing over the top about this track. One of the best Harrison songs.    Maxwell's Silver Hammer-The first novelty song. A tale of a serial killer who hits people over the head, told in a light and amusing way. This song is ok, it's fine....the first few times. After a while it is certainly a track that you are going to want to skip. Even Lennon didn't like it!    Oh! Darling-Sung by McCartney I think his voice sounds great on this, even if Lennon thought that he should have recorded this instead.    Octopus's Garden-Whilst being quite catchy, I really do not like this song. It sounds too cheesy, childish lyrics, adequate vocals and nothing more.    I Want You (She's So Heavy)-Overly long. That's the first thought that comes into my head. It has some good moments but not enough to keep it interesting for 7:47. The guitar intro is great as are some little jam moments, the vocals are also very good throughout. But there is also too much that just doesn't work.    Here Comes The Sun- An amazing song, once again written by George Harrison. From the very start it's beautygrabs you. The guitar throughout is pure perfection, the lyrics shine and gradually the song blossoms into the work of genius that it is. It is both optimistic and uplifting.    Because- It is not the best track, though a very solid one, on the album but it is a great showcase for the band. Stripped of all the trimmings that a lot of bands need to sound good, the fab four deliver amazing vocals and harmonies, only bettered by the Beach Boys. After all, the Beatles were primarily a vocal band and this song clearly reminds us of this. A fine performance.    You Never Give Me Your Money- A lovely song, sung in McCartney's best voice. The piano in the first half is great, the harmony in the second half very pretty and the guitar is once again brilliant yet not overdone.    Sun King- Sit back and relax, here comes the mid-album chill out zone. The first minute sounds similar to Fleetwood Mac's Albatross.    Mean Mr Mustard- Despite being a novelty song, it is much better than the previous two. Plus, it is only a minute long. Quite catchy, silly lyrics, nothing terrible here and nice little jaunt that quickly leads into...    Polythene Pam- One of the few times I actually notice John's accent. A daft song with a great intro that bursts open from the end of the previous track. Again, only a short song but very catchy.    She Came In Through the Bathroom Window- Written by Paul about a fan that did what the title says. This is a great follow on from Polythene Pam, the change is fantastic.    Golden Slumbers- Soaring strings, soft piano, gentle bass; a wonderful start to this song. Then we get Paul's top-notch vocals come in during the chorus. Another gentle dip and then it goes straight into...    Carry That Weight- `You never give me your money' makes a comeback halfway in, with different lyrics, then back to Carry That Weight and then, without looking backwards we plunge into....    The End- The band on flawless form; guitars, vocals, melody and Ringo all merge into one great showmanship of their abilities. The last great piece of music they laid down. For many this is the highlight of the entire album and it is kind of hard to disagree with them.    Her Majesty- 23 seconds long and a novelty song once again. I don't see the point of this and certainly it's position on the album. We've just heard a magnificent medley of several songs and then the last track would have been an awesome and perfect end to the album and then this gets put in right at the end.

0446677450|3.0|As a long-time critic of the self-help movement--its \\"get financial freedom now!\\" wing in particular--I have mixed emotions about this book, which I've read twice now, and to which I've devoted considerable thought. On the one hand, there's no question that it presents certain core financial principles that are beyond dispute (at least in a free-market society). Among them is Kiyosaki's bedrock argument, which is that those who put their money to work for them will realize larger, faster returns than those who simply work for money. And, the book is an entertaining read, with plenty of real-life anecdotes to enliven what would otherwise be dry advice.    <br /> <br />On the other hand, this book--as much as anything to come out of the wealth-building genre--exemplifies the key problem with the movement as a whole: the fact that most people cannot do what the author would have them do. Understand, it's not a matter of choice or \\"will\\"; it's the (basically) closed-system nature of the market itself. We simply can't all be chiefs, owning our own businesses and/or letting our investments or OPM (other people's money) work for us. There is room at the top for only a handful of such people. In fact--and by definition--those who truly succeed at Kiyosaki's system DEPEND on the fact that most other people will NOT embrace that system. (If that were not the case, the whole plan would collapse.) Now, one could argue that by reading this book, you can become one of the handful...and that's fine. Just understand that the odds are against you. <br /> <br />I recently wrote a review of Dave Barry's new book, Money Secrets. (That's the main reason I reread Kiyosaki's book, for comparison purposes, and as a \\"refresher course.\\") Though Barry writes with tongue-in-cheek, and intends his book as satire, I honestly feel that you're better off with Barry than with Kiyosaki. And no, as DB himself might put it, I am not making that up...

0375831002|5.0|\\"A human doesn't have a heart like mine. The human heart is a line, whereas my own is a circle, and I have the endless ability to be in the right place at the right time. The consequence of this is that I'm always finding humans at their best and worst. I see their ugly and their beauty, and I wonder how the same thing can be both.\\"    So muses the narrator of Markus Zusak's powerful and moving new novel, THE BOOK THIEF. As you might guess, this is no ordinary narrator. The contemplative first person guiding you through this book is Death, an at-once fitting and ironic vanguard for a tale that both celebrates the power of words and agonizes over the consequences of their use.    Set against the tragedy-stained canvas of World War II, Death tells the story of young Liesel Meminger (the eponymous book thief) growing up in Nazi Germany under the watchful eye of a staunch foster mother and kindly foster father who teaches her to read. She attends meetings of the BDM, a youth group aimed at indoctrinating young girls into Hitler's ideology. She plays soccer with the boys on her street, holding her own in any disputes that arise. And all the while, the dreams of her dead brother haunt and goad her into a fascination with reading and words that inevitably leads to her life of crime.    It is a meeting with Max Vandenburg, a 24-year-old Jewish man being hidden in Liesel's basement by her compassionate foster parents, that alters the course of Liesel's life. Max, too, is haunted by nightmares of a family he lost in the harrowing aftermath of Kristallnacht. Together, Max and Liesel discover a shared love of words that leads to a decisive understanding about the role words play in both bravery and cowardice. Each, in their own way, sets out to use this knowledge to shape the world around them.    While other writers have employed Death as a narrator, Zusak makes his own indelible mark on the technique in the dimensions he gives to the character. Death is simultaneously dispassionate about his work and the impact it can have while striving to understand humanity's resilience. Death boasts an omniscience of what will happen in life but also a naivety about what can happen in the human heart.    In the ultimate expression of his dichotomous theme, Zusak creates a touching love letter to books and writing, framed in arguably the most horrific period in human history. But his greatest triumph is delivering a reminder that no writer enters this world quietly. Writers are born of eruptions and detonations, and the truly exceptional ones, like Zusak, continue to channel these explosive energies to craft a truly remarkable book that will be admired for generations.       --- Reviewed by Brian Farrey [...]

The first time I heard this song I thought to myself that System could have written an entire album using this single theme a la Tull's \\"Thick as a Brick\\" or \\"Passion Play.\\"  In any event this is great music. B000E6UK5K|1.0|First off, I'm an enthusiastic Floydophile and David Gilmour fan. The guy has some of the best guitar chops in rock and roll history, and his \\"About Face\\" album remains the finest solo project any member of the band has yet produced ... including that of the mega-talented acid casualty Syd Barrett. <br /> <br />However, in all fairness and honesty, I must say \\"On An Island\\" is the single worst thing I have ever heard any of the Pink Floyd musicians do in his career. In fact, this record is embarrassingly bad. Let's start with the insipid lyrics. I know that singing about the albatross hanging \\"motionless upon the air\\" wasn't exactly a high point on \\"Echoes,\\" but what are we to make of lines like this? <br /> <br />\\"I'll make my getaway <br />Time on my own <br />Search for a better way <br />To find my way home to your smile.\\" <br /> <br />Or this from \\"Where We Start\\": <br /> <br />\\"I light a campfire away from the path. <br />We lie in the bluebells, a woodpecker laughs.\\" <br /> <br />Huh? I mean, seriously, WTF? A child of 10 could come up with better words than that. <br /> <br />Then we've got the, um, instrumental part of the record, which consists of a LOT of aimless guitar noodling and emotionally flat pseudo-orchestral self-indulgence. It's as if Gilmour is determined to submerge his gift for expressing simple melodies so that he can show off his knowledge of classical arrangements and motifs. Perhaps it's no coincidence that the only half-decent tunes on the whole album are \\"Take A Breath\\" and \\"This Heaven,\\" both of which benefit enormously from having Phil Manzanera on keyboards and piano, respectively. Heck, at least Manzanera supplies some of the much-needed texture this record otherwise entirely lacks. <br /> <br />Now, I don't know what the reviewers who give this record four or five stars have been smoking, but there is simply no way \\"On An Island\\" deserves even a semi-respectable three-star rating. And lest you suspect I am being too hard on Gilmour, let me just say that I generally respect artists who challenge their listeners and disregard their fan base to do something different. Whether it's Ray Charles releasing modern sounds in country music, or Cowboy Junkies experimenting with grunge, or even Bob Dylan going electric, there is much to be said for allowing a person the freedom to pursue his or her own vision. I don't dislike this work because it's a departure from a Pink Floyd concept album. It's more a matter of Gilmour's apparent contempt for his audience, or his essential laziness, that bothers me here. He's not even trying anymore. The entire track list may be exquisitely produced, but it's inconsequential stuff that comes across as the kind of canned spam he phoned in to meet a deadline or pay for a third house out in the country. <br /> <br />The young David Gilmour dabbled with psychedelics, played around with LSD and mushrooms, and helped create some of the most glorious space rock of his generation. Geez, he and his fellow Floydians practically pioneered a new genre of popular music. The old David Gilmour writes songs like a man whose idea of recreational drug use is limited to Maalox and Quaaludes, and who inspires nothing so much as a world-weary boredom and ennui with life and all it has to offer a multi-millionaire with way too much time on his hands.  <br /> <br />Sorry Dave, but if this is all you've got left, Roger Waters has nothing to worry about in his solo career.

0307263118|5.0|Let's see, what have we here? This little book seems to have caused quite a stir, I suppose because of its winning the Man Booker.  On the one side, we have those agreeing with New York Times Ubercritic, Ms Kachutani, who penned a searing review, accusing the writer of \\"navel-gazing\\" and the committee's passing over of a \\"more vibrant\\" novel.  On the other hand, it seems to me, we have lovers of the English language and lyrical writing. And, somewhere along the way, someone writing for The Daily Telegraph in England has compared Banville to Nabokov. I suppose I'll just weigh in on what I think of this conflict, briefly, and then give my own impressions.<br /><br />First off, this Nabokov thing:  I don't know who started it, but it really won't do. The two writers are at opposite poles of the writing sphere, and this book is the last place for a Nabokov devotee to waste his/her time trying to find him resurrected. As for Ms Kachutani's, remarks, they only add clearer evidence to what Harold Bloom stated years ago; to wit, that The New York Times is becoming the most unliterary paper in the nation. \\"Vibrant\\", trendy novels are all good and well, but, in the end, almost always ephemeral and remaindered and forgotten in the span of hardly any time at all. Great lyrical writings are the treasures of our culture and deserve to be rewarded whenever possible; they are our language's true inheritance and will be treasured by lovers of the language for years.....Banville's novel is such a work.<br /><br />Preliminaries out of the way, the book reminded me, from the start, of the work of Sir Thomas Browne, whom Banville obviously borrowed from, and whose character, Max, mentions once. His work is replete with words that will leave even the most erudite running to their unabridged OEDs on nearly every page, and whose work is filled with such apothegms as, \\"This world has always seemed to me a hospital.\\" The similarities are unmistakable. - Also, of course, Proust - Proust is really the artist that The Daily Telegraph writer should have mentioned as Banville's literary forbear.<br /><br />Nevertheless, this is an odd little book, and needs to be read several times to be appreciated fully.  What I mean is, after we learn the basic surface facts of the mystery that are finally divulged to us in the last few pages then yes, that's resolved. But the greater mystery of the book and the subject it tackles, the human condition, remain muddled.  From the time he \\"walked into the sea\\" when his wife died, and immediately sunk in his \\"bathysphere\\", a suit for diving into the deepest part of the ocean for underwater observation, to the ending, we get a beautiful, skewed view of life, love, and memory.  What of these deeper mysteries? I, for one, truly don't know, and I don't think Banville does either. But I feel that each time I reread this book I'm closer to some sort of clearer resolution on the subject, at least as seen by Max.  This focus on the depths and ability to bring out in us the compulsion to reread is to my mind, part of qualifying for the laurel of great literature, a test which Max and his \\"bathyspheric\\" observations pass easily.  There is a part of me that wishes Banville had expanded a bit more and made the novel longer.  But, as Max says, early in the book, quoting Valery, \\"there is no finishing of a work, only the abandoning of it.\\" Lovers of literature will find themselves unable to abandon this work for some time.s really the artist that The Daily Telegraph writer should have mentioned as Banville's literary forbear. <br /> <br />Nevertheless, this is an odd little book, and needs to be read several times to be appreciated fully. 0307276902|4.0|A Million Little Pieces by James Frey is an insightful, descriptive story about a mans grueling experience with addictions. Portrayed as real experiences with some fabricated key parts, Frey's A Million Littler Pieces is the story of his life. Despite the non-fiction, or lack of in this book the basic emotions he presents are real. His struggles and attempts to cope with cravings are very much relatable with everyone who has been in that situation. All the events in the book might of not happened to Frey as he wrote it, but could very much be true to anyone else. His sentences, stanzas, and the way he uses repetitive words is key to capture readers into Frey's warped mental state of mind. This created me to linger on the hook of the chain of repeating words, long enough to understand and analyze the importance of each one. <br /> <br />I think the most common misconception of this book, is that it's all about him doing a bunch of notorious drugs. This is not true. The opening of the book focuses on James confusion on how he got where he was, and what were the previous events that lead him there. Throughout the novel, we slowly learn his whereabouts of his past through him reminiscing about the earlier years. It's quite easy to read James feelings, but Frey gives no simple, one answer explanation to addictions, or why they start, and how to conquer it. The book outlines James life in rehab and his struggles with himself, and others around him. It shows the reader how powerful dependence on a substance can be, and how it can tear someone's life apart. <br /> <br />In the end, just as I expected, there is no fairy tale endings. After the story ends, there's a list of character names, with their outcome. This captures the realism of everything when it shows readers that despite rehab, many relapse, and die. A Million Little Pieces doesn't preach to not use drugs, nor dose it glorify drug use. It simply states the possibilities drugs can have on ones life, and leaves readers to decide for themselves if it's all worth it or not. This book wont be as satisfying to everyone. But for someone who has seen or dealt with cravings, and lack of self control with anything, you can see a part of yourself in James. And because of that I couldn't help myself but cross my fingers, and hope that James will be fine in the end.

B000E5N62U|5.0|According to Morphy's Law, anything that can go right, will.  <br /> <br />With Morph the Cat, Donald Fagen continues with the momentum of  Steely Dan`s recent reactivation, this time, with another outstanding solo release--his third, and first since 1993's Kamakiriad. <br /> <br />Will anyone who's not already familiar with the work of Fagen--by himself, and with Walter Becker, of Steely Dan's significant contribution to  recorded music--use this album as an introduction to his work? I suspect not. But I still hope so: this member of the Rock and Roll Hall of Fame is there rightfully, and Morph the Cat offers corroborating evidence for the justice of his selection. <br /> <br />Surprises? Few.  Some--here, and elsewhere--have bemoaned a manner of predictability observed in this recording.  I'd offer--as a decades-long and dedicated fan of Donald and Walter, apart and separately--that predictability is a right fine thing.  Now in his late 50s, Donald Fagen has his manner of craft dialed in. <br /> <br />What to expect? (for those who don't already know?) Songcraft that reveals a deep fondness for the pop songform; an unusual gift for melody; textured horn and backing vocal arrangements; and a lyrical wit dryer than hot laundry lint. <br /> <br />Personal highpoints include: <br /> <br />The absolutely lovely and heartfelt tip of the hat the recently-passed Ray Charles: a blues verse that breaks free of the 12-bar format for the chorus, Fagen seems to give some due thanks as he works his way through a continued need to self-efface; <br /> <br />A tip of hat to same absolutely chilling and gorgeous harmonica by Howard Levy--it presents surprising and elegant flourishes; <br /> <br />American culture has not comfortably made a place for death. Fagen, though, dresses him in white in Brite Nitegown, offers three vignettes of passing on, and sets it to a manner of funky bump shuffle that few besides Fagen are capable of.  Healthy. Fun. Genius.  <br /> <br />Without namingany specific presidents that any of us may or may not have voted for, In Mary Shut the Garden Door, Fagen paints an image of individual choices and behaviors in response to the political and social climate we now find ourselves in.  To me, this is a terribly sad, powerful piece.  <br /> <br />Fortunately, the album wraps with a reprise of the title track to shift the mood from what would have other wise been a somber emotional conclusion.  While this native New Englander and lifelong Red Sox fan will have a very hard time singing along to Lyrics that include \\"Blessed Yankees,\\" I'm grateful for this reprise. For the melody. For the return to the top.  <br /> <br />I am among those who see every Dan and solo project release as a gift, knowing faithfully that it will not stray from principles of songwriting and production excellence that the track record has established. I'll refrain from making comparisons from Morph the Cat to other works (solo or as Steely Dan). Fellow  dedicated fans will find their own moments of recollection, similarity, and connection.  I can say, without naming titles,  that I absolutely detect harmonic, rhythmic, and thematic ties that bind this work within an evolutionary path that includes Donald Fagen's work as a solo artist and as a member of Steely Dan.  Morph the Cat sits very comfortably alongside and among a well-established body of work.

B000E8QVWY|4.0|<br />WALK THE LINE, the Johnny Cash-June Carter story, is an exhilarating musical biography.  It starts in 1968 inside Folsom Prison, flashes back to Cash's childhood in the Deep South of the early 1950's, then works its way back up to 1968 and beyond.  With brilliant performances by Joaquin Phoenix (Oscar nominee) and Reese Witherspoon (Oscar winner), Cash's life and music career come alive.  Both actors spent months learning how to do their own singing and guitar playing live before an audience, and it shows in the electrifying musical numbers. <br /> <br />Director/co-writer James Mangold filmed the movie on location in Memphis, Nashville, and Arkansas along (I think) the Mississippi River.  The 1950's and 1960's sets and costumes, from my own childhood era, are perfection and should have been Oscar-nominated if they weren't.  The film runs 135 minutes, but between the concert scenes and period re-creation, I never wanted it to end.  If WALK THE LINE has any flaw, in fact, it is that it could have lasted longer for me and didn't just put three more decades of narrative crawls against a black finale background.  I wanted more music and more of the perfect Phoenix and Witherspoon performances. <br /> <br />This may be one of the finest musical biographies I have ever seen.  The DVD includes a directors' commentary and 10 deleted scenes that help clarify scene and character. WALK THE LINE is a beautifully crafted labor of love movie gem.  It particularly deserved the Sound Mixing Oscar nomination for spectacular concert scenes.  The movie plays and sounds, inexplicably, way better on DVD than in a theater. It is an absolute must-see. NOTE: Made in Panavision; buy or rrent the widescreen version.  Amazon.com is selling it at half-price. <br /> <br />

B000BW7QWW|5.0|I watched Firefly when it was on TV and own the DVD set so I was excited about the movie and had high expectations. Those expectations were met, even if they werent exceeded. I want to make this review for people who are not familiar with the show. It helps if you're a science fiction fan, but it's not required. Essentially Serenity is a space opera that blends the setting of the old west with the far future. Sounds like a gimmick? Well it isn't. Why isn't it? Because it's done right.     The way the story blends the various elements make it believable that there are some well established planets that are futuristic are \\"Star Trek\\"-like and there are other planets that have hardly been colonized, thus more like frontier land. This is where our heroes come from. It's easier to understand and get that feel if you have seen the TV series, but you get enough exposition to keep the new viewers from looking at the movie cross-eyed.     The script is, of course, solid. Joss Whedon is known throughout the entertainment industry as being a very good writer. He alone turned a silly B-movie (Buffy the Vampire Slayer) into one of the best-written series on television. Joss' script for Serenity is no exception. You get a good mix of plot twists, comical moments, and drama peppered throughout a story that starts off simple then opens up pretty big. The only negative I will give on the writing is the movie has the pacing and feel of a television show. It reminds me of the same feel I got from Star Trek: Insurrection. It's not necessarily a bad thing. It's just an approach to the movie that's different than what I'm used to from a feature film. Don't let that keep you from seeing Serenity. If it's like a TV show, it's like the best TV show you will ever see on the big screen.     While this movie only had a budget about $40 million you wouldn't know by looking at it. They put that money on some fantastic special effects. The scenes in space are beautiful to behold, both the calm ones and the massively large ship battles. By the way I think Serenity does a better job with the big space battle than Star Wars Ep3 (same crazy action, but nowhere near as distracting). I think what helped on the budget was many of the sets were already built from the television show. Still the audio and added effects do stand out in a very good way. The acting is so solid you will find yourself liking more than one character in the movie. The music is a nice blend of that space opera soundtrack and old west.     This DVD includes extras like deleted scenes, outtakes, and some documentaries. The deleted scenes are nice. Some of them are so good I really hope Joss decides to do a director's cut DVD and put some of those scenes back in. The bloopers/outtakes are so-so. It has some funny off-the-cuff kind of stuff, but mostly it's the actors forgetting lines and cursing about it (it's worth noting the outtakes should be considered at the VERY least PG-13, if not R, when watching with the kids due to language). The documentaries are a mixed bag for me. Having seen the documentaries in the Firefly DVD I can tell these are lacking somewhat. They are still good documentaries. Just not as good as on the TV DVD set.     The introduction by Joss Whedon is the one they played at the pre-screenings so it won't really hit home to the new viewer as it does the fan. Personally I can do without it. Too evangelitical. There is a commentary track by Joss also, which is much better than the introduction. He gives a lot of director-type information like lighting and what camera lenses they used, but mentions nothing in lines of background info on the story. Joss also self-depreciates himself a lot on the commentary. First its entertaining that he can crack wise on himself, but it eventually gets old.     This is a great DVD for any science-fiction fan. You get a good story, great action, sweet special effects, and some nice extra material to boot. If you got caught in the middle of the whole Brownshirt fiasco and ended up resenting ever watching this film youre doing yourself a disservice. Forget the fanboys and take the movie for what it is a good sci-fi adventure.     HD-DVD Version    You get the same cool movie with a heck of a higher resolution. The down side is it looks like you lose some of the bonus features. The documentaries are still there, but it seems like maybe the outtakes and deleted scenes aren't. Don't ask me why they wouldn't just put it in. There should be plenty of room on the new technology. If they do indeed have all of the special features found in the original DVD on this HD-DVD version than by all means go for the higher resolution (if you have a HD-DVD player). Otherwise wait until a better version comes along.

B0006GAXAA|5.0|Below you will find a lengthy analysis of one song on this record \\"I want you back.\\" If a single song can generate this kind of response, imagine the musical content of the entire 21 song record. <br /> <br />This song obliterates me. All the performances are incredible, including, to quote a friend, Michael's astounding, dumbfounding lead vocal. <br /> <br />The bass part sounds to me as if it came straight from the composer and arranger, but in any matter it's stuffed full with inventive ideas and super fonky! Given the calculated nature of this recording I would expect the arranger to have worked out the bass part note for note. The recording is a careful tapestry of many sounds that blend together to give the overall effect. The intro is a good example. The melody played by the bass is wonderful, but that melody is doubled by other instruments plus other things are woven around that melody, all indicating that the arranger wrote it all out note for note, the bass player serving to faithfully play the arrangement. <br /> <br />(Un)fortunately, any excellence in other portions of this song is obscured by Michael's unbelievable vocal. So much stuff, so much soul, so much range and flexibility. This vocal is way in the unbelievable range for any artist. But to come from a brand new 11 year old is, as my friend put it so well, \\"dumbfounding.\\" (If this selection does not feature sound clips, look at other Jackson 5 records here on Amazon to hear the mentioned snippets.) <br />*He sounds a little hoarse and that increases the authority.  <br />*The way he uses subtle, rhythmic vibrato on the word \\"me\\" in the phrase \\"won't you please let me back in your heart.\\" (Hear this on the Amazon snippet.) <br />*The pause and surprise attack on the word \\"back\\" in the same phrase. (Hear this on the Amazon snippet.) <br />*The screaming with 100% authority.  <br />*The drifting out of tune on the words \\"now, baby, yeah\\" in the phrase \\"Yes I do now, Oh oh baby, yeah yeah yeah yeah,Nah Nah no no.\\" (Is there a technical term for this \\"drifting out of tune\\" vocal technique?) (Hear this on the Amazon snippet.) <br />*The authority and inventiveness and soul of the scatting on that same phrase and other scats in the song, including the various fun \\"Huh\\"s. <br />*The natural way he does the call and response sections with his brothers. <br />*The perfect timing all over the place, including jumping on some of the responses a little early. <br />*The exciting vocal build on the phrase \\"All I want, all I need, all I want, all I need!\\" with the fantastic atonal exclamation on the second \\"all I want.\\" <br />And on and on. You could almost analyze every single note the guy sings. The fact that this guy could do this at 11 years old shows beyond any question that singing at this level requires talent given to you directly by God. You can't get it and you can't learn it. God either gave it to you or He didn't. My guess is that some genius worked out most of this stuff and recorded a track for Michael to sing along with during the session. I mean God given talent or not, Michael could not have come up with that stuff on his own, atonal, drifting out of tune, etc. But to even sing along and get these results....incredible. Plus, who was that singing genius that came up with all that stuff? <br /> <br />Other great parts of the song are the pervasive background vocals, the excellent call and response, the subtle, almost inaudible use of strings and background vocalizing, and the various guitar parts that weave an interesting tapestry. <br /> <br />One thing that I think is ground breaking but at the same time takes away from this arrangement is the calculated, restrained drum part. The drum part sounds completely canned, written out and played note for note. It's not exuberant; instead it's carefully restrained. To my ears it sounds too mellow for this joyous, raucous romp. But it's groundbreaking because this style of drumming became prevalent later and today in the 2000s you rarely hear any drum pyrotechnics in favor of a steady drum beat that does not break the flow of the song. <br /> <br />I wonder if all the vocals on this recording are Jacksons? I assume they are and that shows the talent of this family and the quality of this product. <br /> <br />Excellence and analysis of each individual part aside, the real value of this song, besides Michael's vocal which you simply can not retire to the background, is the overall sound. Each part is carefully calculated and blended to create a unique, exuberant, joyful, new, and instantly recognizable sound that's unlike anything that had been heard before. Though each part is worthy of study, perhaps the best way to listen to this song is with a 6 inch speaker through an AM radio in your car. And isn't that the way it was meant to be? <br /> <br />Larry Brown <br /> <br />

B000E5N62U|2.0|I'm a big fan--have been since the 70's.  I'm also a trained musician who has taught in a conservatory and made a living doing film-scores.  I mention this because it partly explains my minority view, here.  I try to listen technically--and I don't mean listen to audio fidelity or the mix--which is, of course, impeccable.    This is a dull album.  It doesn't improve with repeated listenings.  It gets more painful.   Repeat:  This is dull.    main problem:  the grooves.  excepting Nightgown, there's very little lift or snap to the grooves.  the session players are competent pros, and they play like it--nothing more.   (Poor communication between player and composer?  who knows)    second problem:  slack-ish lyrics.  nothing in the same league with previous efforts.    third problem: plenty of chord-changes, but to little effect.  craft but no excitement.    fourth problem:  not funky, but also not rock-y.  solos merely careful and competent.  this isn't jazz, it's not funk, it's not rock, it's just...careful.    fifth problem: too much medium-tempo plodding, with little contrast in tempos.    sixth problem:  lack of hooks    seventh problem: lack of space, or use of silence--things are filled-in, but again, to little effect    Pagoda has a long line and interesting Lydian-ish chord changes, but a contrived long line is not the same thing as an inspired melody that soars because the composer felt it that way.      this is mastered loud, but 'loud' is not 'powerful'    this is dull, dull, dull.

I truly enjoyed the stories of Liam Clancy, Pete Seeger, Joan Baez, and so many others who have known Dylan from the beginning of his career.  The plethora of rare old film footage was definitely a bonus.  The chronicle of his early Britain tour seemed to expose a vulnerability in the young Dylan that I had never seen before.  I found this to be quite poignant.    (This has no particular bearing on the film, but... I was taken aback at the end of the 2nd DVD, about half way through the credits, by Dylan's \\"Lay Down Your Weary Tune\\", sung from beginning to end. This song has been running through my head since Hurricane Katrina devastated the Gulf Coast. The lyrics are especially powerful to me, and this ended my experience on a sweetly emotional note.)        I highly recommend this DVD set for everyone who is interested in the evolution of music and Bob Dylan's essential role in that evolution.  Kudos to Martin Scorsese for doing this powerful film... a true work of art.  It left me wanting more... I hope Mr. Scorsese will pick up where he left off in a future documentary... there are so many more years to cover.  Oh, and Mr. Dylan, I do hope you returned those Woodie Guthrie records you \\"borrowed\\"!

0553801503|5.0|The War of the Five Kings is drawing to a close, while an even greater war is brewing. \\"A Feast for Crows\\" takes place during this slow period between the two major wars. Martin's fourth \\"Ice and Fire\\" novel shares more similarities with his first than it does with his second or third. Just as \\"A Game of Thrones\\" was mostly about setting things up for the next two novels, so is this one. <br /> <br />Which isn't to say the book is uneventful, but if you're expecting it to be as action-packed as \\"A Storm of Swords\\" you'll be disappointed. There's still plenty going on to make the book interesting. Dorne's responding to the murder of Prince Oberyn, Riverrun is under siege from the Freys, Storm's End is under siege from the Tyrells, the Iron Islands are struggling to appoint a new Lord, the Vale is reacting to the murder of its Lady, and the people of Highgarden are struggling to deal with the Queen Regent. Though Tommen sits on the Iron Throne, Cersei Lannister is the one truly ruling the realm. <br /> <br />Martin's writing style - already great in the past books - has gotten even better in this one. His style is often elegant and clever at the same time. Like all the other books, \\"Feast\\" features an ensemble cast, but it's notable how the series has gradually moved away from the Starks since beginning. The first book spent about 80% of its time on House Stark and its members. This latest book spends at most 15% of its time on the Starks. <br /> <br />The only major complaint I have is with Brienne. Except for her very last one, Brienne's chapters are a waste of time and paper. Whereas, all the other characters were very gripping and entertaining, Brienne was downright boring. It didn't take long for me to get so bored with her chapters, that I started skipping them. After I finished the book, I went back and read them all, and well, I'm really not sorry for skipping them. <br /> <br />More than a few reviewers have complained about the fact that this volume is missing characters, but those who were following along on Martin's site know there's good reason for that. By the time A Feast for Crows reached 1300 manuscript pages in length, Martin still wasn't finished with the story. The story was far too big to publish in one book, so Martin's only options were: a) to cut A Feast for Crows right at the mid-way point and publish it Volumes 1 & 2, or b) to cut out some of the characters' stories and move them into A Dance with Dragons. Every good story requires a beginning, a middle, a climax, and an ending; had Martin had taken the first approach, the story would have a beginning and a middle, but no climax and no real ending. At least with this approach, the story has *some* closure. So, yes, the story is missing characters - Tyrion, Dany, Jon, Bran, Davos, and Stannis, among others - but they'll be back next year for A Dance with Dragons, the parallel novel to A Feast for Crows. <br /> <br />Overall, this is one of the greatest books I've ever read. Five years was a long time to wait, but it was well worth it. H.L. Mencken once said, \\"The primary aim of the novel, at all times and everywhere, is the representation of human beings at their follies and villainies, and no other art form clings to that aim so faithfully.\\" His quote perfectly captures the underlying theme of Martin's series. At heart, A Song of Ice and Fire is about war and human brutality; about how so many want power, and the terrible things they'll do to achieve it, sending thousands of men to their deaths and bringing death and destruction upon a whole country just to achieve power.  <br />

0393061310|4.0|\\"Guns, Germs, and Steel\\" provides a very interesting perspective on history and helps explain why the West has come to dominate the world.  However, in the process he strains to avoid including the impact of anything possibly \\"racist\\" - eg. differences in culture or physiology.<br /><br />Diamond's main point is that ability to cultivate crops and tend herds (determined by geography and available native species) allows setting aside resources for innovation and war.  Germs acquired from domestic animals then played decisive roles in the European conquests of Native Americans, Australians, South Africans, and Pacific Islanders.  Animal-based means of transporting food surpluses were also a pre-requisite for development of technologically innovative societies.<br /><br />Factors Encouraging Farming:  Declining availability of wild foods, development of methods to harvest (eg. flint blades attached to handles, for harvesting; baskets; mortars and pestles to remove husks).  Cereals were a first crop.  The best area for this at the time was the Fertile Crescent.<br /><br />Factors in Domesticating Animals:  Carnivores are too inefficient, want a fast growth rate (rules out monkeys, apes, elephants), easy breeding (rules out vicunas), good disposition (rules out African buffalo, hippos, zebras), limited panic tendency (rules out gazelles), herdable (rules out antelope - males fight when confined).<br /><br />Another key Diamond point is that domesticated plants spread faster across Europe-Asia because of the long distances at similar latitudes.  Conversely, Africa and the America's longest distances are primarily north-south, posing greater difficulty because of the great climate differences.  In addition, the dry Texas area acted as a barrier between the SE and SW U.S. for transmitting crop-growing lessons.<br /><br />Diamond then concludes that the long East-West axis across Europe-Asia helped spread the development of the wheel for transporting crops, and writing (helped in trade).  Meanwhile, the prehistoric Mexico's wheel and early writing systems never made it to the Andes.<br /><br />Some areas (eg. Australia, and the Western U.S.) did not have readily domesticatable crops.<br /><br />Major killers of humanity throughout recent history - smallpox, flu, TB, malaria, plague, measles, and cholera - are infectious diseases evolved from diseases of animals, according to Diamond.  Until WWII, more victims of war died of war-borne microbes than battle wounds.  Diamond goes on to assert that agriculture led to greater disease because of higher population densities, use of feces as fertilizer, disease transmitting rodents attracted by stored food, and transmission along resulting trade routes.<br /><br />Smallpox gave the Spaniards in America a decisive advantage - from 1520 to 1618 Mexico's population fell from about 20 million to about 1.6 million.  For the New World as a whole, the Indian population decline in the two centuries post-Columbus was about 95%.  Indians did not have diseases to transmit back to Europe because they had much fewer animals, did not drink their milk, and did not keep them indoors.  (Eurasia had 13 species of big domestic mammals - some also useful for cultivation, and the Americas just one very local species - the llama, confined to a small area in South America.)  However, tropical Asia, Africa, Indonesia, and New Guinea had cholera and yellow fever - making those areas much more difficult to colonize.<br /><br />Acceptance of change is another important factor - Islam, until about 1350 led Europe, until its religion began holding it back.  (Diamond also mentions later that inhabitants of the Fertile Crescent undid their agricultural advantage by cutting down trees, allowing the sandy soil to become a major enemy.)  Similarly, China formerly led the world in technology and ship-building, but then regressed under subsequent inward-focused leadership.<br /><br />Food production increases population size andalso acts to make societies more complex.  For example, Diamond believes that a large society that leaves conflict resolution to members is guaranteed to blow up.  A second reason involves economic and welfare considerations - goods in excess of an individual's needs must be transferred to a centralized authority, which then redistributes these to individuals with deficits.<br /><br />Overall, Guns, Germs, and Steel\\" provides interesting insights into early world history; however, I strongly suspect Diamond's efforts to be politically correct limited his insights.also acts to make societies more complex.  For example, Diamond believes that a large society that leaves conflict resolution to members is guaranteed to blow up. The Long Halloween is something that I've been meaning to read for some time now, because it is considered by many to be one of the greatest Batman stories in existence, which is a true honor (from what I've heard, there are some amazing Batman books as well as some that should be burned).  At long last, I finally read The Long Halloween, and I couldn't be happier. <br />Some spoilers to follow <br />Taking place very early on in Batman's career, The Long Halloween tells the story of a serial killer known as Holiday.  Starting on October 31st and ending on the following October 31st, Holiday kills every month on a national holiday, including Thanksgiving, Christmas, St. Patrick's Day, and Independence Day.  Most of the targets are associates and family members of Carmine \\"The Roman\\" Falcone (introduced in Frank Miller's Batman: Year One).  Falcone is the mob boss currently in control of the crime-ridden Gotham City, and it appears that someone is out to end his reign.  Batman, Police Captain James Gordan, and District Attorney Harvey Dent have formed an alliance to put an end to Falcone's empire themselves, but Holiday is making things tough for them, because as much as they want Falcone out, they want it done legally. <br />Furthermore, the emerging supervillains that eventually become Batman's rogues' gallery begin giving Batman some trouble.  The Joker claims that Gotham isn't big enough for two psychotic murderers and, in an attempt to kill Holiday, tries to release his toxic gas on a crowded square in the center of downtown Gotham, while Catwoman tends to show up and ruin things for Batman whenever he gets close to Falcone.  It doesn't help that Bruce Wayne and Selina Kyle (Batman and Catwoman) are in a relationship, and yet neither knows that they are fighting each other when they meet as their alter egos.  Even Falcone hires villains like Poison Ivy, the Riddler, and the Scarecrow to get some of his dirty work done.  And by the end, Two-Face is born from Harvey Dent, taking Batman's friend away forever. <br />The Long Halloween serves as the transition of power from the mob goons to the supervillains in Gotham as well as a retelling of Two-Face's origin.  It is also a very well-told noir detective story (after all, Batman is the world's greatest detective).  Jeph Loeb masterfully tells his story of Dent's descent into madness, the fall of Falcone, and the very gray nature of Batman and Gordan's \\"victory\\".  The twists are also completely unexpected.  Just when you think that you've figured out who Holiday is, the rug is pulled out from under you (I thought I figured it out, and I sort of did, until the final twist is revealed).  Furthermore, the art is perfect for the book.  When I first picked up The Long Halloween, I was a little put off by the art, but as I started to read the book, I realized that the style of art perfectly fit the story. <br />This is a must-read for any Batman fan.  It is a great story for anyone; no prior knowledge of the characters is required (in fact, it helps set up certain characters' personalities), and it involves nearly every major Batman villain imaginable.  Also, this is one of the stories that served as inspiration for the amazing Batman Begins film.  Batman doesn't get much better than this.

B00005JOWR|5.0|\\"I front an organization that kills 1,200 people a day,\\" says Nick Taylor (Aaron Eckhart), a lobbyist for the tobacco industry. His job is to stand up for tobacco companies that are being attacked by knuckleheads who accuse the businesses of being murdering bastards. Talyor loves what he does, and he's very good at it. He is able to attack his opponents from every angle imaginable. But as clever as he is, he never observes the fact that smoking is essentially long term suicide, and that some people who do smoke are too dumb to make an attempt to quit.  <br /> <br />As a conservative Republican, I think that the attack on the tobacco industry is just as ridiculous as the attacks on gun manufacturers and McDonald's. Some may remember the outrageous lawsuit a number of years ago in which a woman spilled hot McDonald's coffee all over herself, sued the establishment, and won. But it get's better. Now obese people are taking McDonald's to court. Obese people aren't obese because of McDonald's. They are obese because they choose not to burn away the calories or because of their inescapable genetic makeup. In \\"Super Size Me,\\" the great 2004 documentary by filmmaker Morgan Spurlock, we were educated on the theory that there is a point where corporate responsibility ends and personal responsibility begins. Corporate responsibility ends at the cash register, and personal responsiblity begins when you digest the food.  <br /> <br />The same aspect applies to the gun industry, and in 2003, the endlessly fascinating \\"Runaway Jury,\\" which was based on a novel by John Grisham, explored lawsuits against the gun industry. Although I disagreed with the outcome, I still gave the film four stars, but the bottom line is simple: companies aren't responsible for what their products do to people, especially by other people. A hopeless dote who blows his co-workers away can't be controled by the gun company that he purchased the firearm from. In the same way, a smoker should not be allowedto sue a tobacco company for hospital treatment for lung cancer. Any judge with a brain would dismiss the case immediately, give the plaintiff a middle finger, and wish him luck on his journey up the creek without a canoe or a paddle.  <br /> <br />\\"Runaway Jury\\" and \\"Super Size Me\\" were answers to the ridiculous arguments against corporations. \\"Thank You For Smoking\\" is more of a hokey outcry rather than an answer. When Nick Taylor stands up for what he believes in, he is more than certain that he is right. He is just as determined as Uri Orlov from last year's excellent \\"Lord of War,\\" who sold illegal arms to every army but the Salvation Army. Taylor is an orator, no more, no less. He is payed to talk, and tobacco companies world wide consider him a go-to man in their time of need.  <br /> <br />\\"Thank You For Smoking\\" draws the curtain on American stupidity, revealing both sides of an argument that will never reach compromise. Taylor's job requires sometimes very dangerous work. He crosses paths with a number of characters in the media and political underworld, much like an assassin would in a gangster film. He is good friends with two very interesting people: Polly Bailey (Maria Bello), an alcoholic who is a lobyist for the alcohol industry, and Jay Bliss (David Koechner), a firearms industry spokesperson. Together, they call themselves The MOD (Merchants Of Death) Squad, meeting in a quiet restaurant for sandwiches and drinks on a regular basis, and discussing marketing their little club with t-shirts and bumper stickers.  <br /> <br />Nick has a son named Joey, played by Cameron Bright, who we saw three weeks ago capping his father and being kidnapped by murderous pedophiles in \\"Running Scared.\\" Here he is being homeschooled by his father, not just in academics, but in the tricks of his father's trade. Nick's boss is BR (J. K. Simmons), an aggressive hot-head who is determined to boost cigarette sales. Nick suggests the movies, in one of the film's most memorable sequences, so that tobacco can begin to appeal to all ages. He also suggests that all of the characters smoke, because, as he so cleverly observes, \\"these days when anybody smokes in the movies, they are either Europeans or psychopaths.\\"  <br /> <br />But wait, there's more. Nick even encounters Heather Holloway (Katie Holmes), a sexy journalist doing an investigaton for The Washington Probe. There is Jeff Megall (Rob Lowe), a corporate mongul who works as a vessel for Nick's ideas. There is Lorne Lutch (Sam Elliott), a former Marlboro Man who is dying of lung cancer and ready to speak out against the tobacco industry. There is also the Captain (Robert Duvall), a tobacco tycoon based in Winston-Salem, North Carolina, who sees Nick as a revolutionary for Amercian society. And then there is the matter of Senator Ortolan Finistirre (William H. Macy), who is quick to attack the tobacco industry from multiple directions but slow to realize that his homestate of Vermont, which produces cheddar cheese, is, in the context of his own arguement, a contributer to cholesterol, the leading cause of death in the United States, something that Nick is educated enough to point out by himself.  <br /> <br />\\"Thank You For Smoking\\" would be the result if Quentin Tarantino were to attempt a social and politcal satire. Since Nick is such an intelligent professional, and because he is able to knock down his enemies one at a time, subtly pinpointing the stupidity of their own argument, the film is wide open for humorous, quotable dialogue that only continues to resonate once it penetrates your eardrums. On the part of writer/director Jason Reitman, no praise is too high. Like Paul Haggis did in devising \\"Crash,\\" Reitman is able to juggle a number of very diverse characters and bring them to life with spirited animation. This is also one of the first satires I've seen in a while in which the humor is never of the goofy slapstick caliber, and seems to emerge so naturally from the material that you almost miss it once it emerges.  <br /> <br />What makes the film so effective is that Nick is an obnoxious jerk that you can't help but adore. He is played with quiet enthusiasm by Aaron Eckhart, an actor who goes shamefully unmentioned when movie snobs gather together to discuss their obsession. He is so effective, with his smirk grin, uplifting voice, and sprawling confidence, that you actually accept him as a lobbyist. Nick belongs there. He has the look and behind-the-scenes personality as a confident professional who takes pleasure in doing his job. Even in handling the character of Nick, the film never goes into the conventional atmosphere of the genre. Unlike in \\"Lord of War,\\" the hero never goes through a period in the third act in which he pays dearly for his sins. Nick makes out like a bandit.  <br /> <br />Jason Reitman has directed a number of short films, but with his first full length project, he has struck gold and set up a mine. The only way to prevent myself from digging into the deeper layers of the film is to end my review. Checking out his resume on imdb.com would lead you to believe that he would never find a niche for himself in the world of filmmaking. Well, he has.  <br /> <br />

They are no longer what I would call \\"progressive rock\\" nor are they \\"neo progressive rock\\". They are simply a pop-rock band, maybe alternative in leaning -- comparable to U2 or Cold Play. They are nothing like IQ, who have remained one of the most amazing bands in the neo-progressive scene. <br /> <br />One problem I have with Marbles is that it jumps on the bandwagon of trite club-drum-machine beats. They are no longer a progressive rock band. Not like they need to have the Tony Banks (or Bach-style) keyboard solos or 25 minute epics. But this is just pop music. Drum beats you can dance to, and song structures that are more like soundscapes than the compositions that prog fans are fond of. You won't find diverse time signatures or abrupt changes. Everything is too smooth... it's slick. Really, Marbles is a great name for this album, since marbles are smooth and slick. <br /> <br />If you are into U2 and Coldplay, you may enjoy Marbles. I'll stick with IQ and Dream Theater... Maybe some day, Marillion will do some serious composing, and come up with work that is both progressive and original. This can be done. But listening to Marbles, I have no desire to listen to any more post-Fish Marillion albums. <br /> <br />It's a shame, because I know these guys are very talented. They can do way better than this repetitive alterna-club pablum. <br /> <br />Maybe they became tired with comparisons to Genesis, Yes, and Pink Floyd. But nowthey sound like U2, Cold Play, and Pink Floyd. Marillion have never been a very original band, but I could always appreciate the strength of Fish's lyrics, and the power with which he sang them. His lyrics were better than anything Gabriel or Collins have written. The lyrics were both real and visceral. They were about the suffering and triumph of regular people with real problems. Never silly or cute, like Genesis lyrics... Instead, they revealed truths about pain, pleasure, and the human psyche. Who can listen to Warm Wet Circles and At That Time of the Night and not be on the brink of weeping?!? <br /> <br />As far as Marillion are concerned, the Fish era material is the best. Neither Fish as a solo artist nor Marillion without Fish can compare. I don't think if Fish re-joined Marillion, it would matter either. Marillion haven't aged well, in my opinion. They have absorbed the lazy influences of contemporary music, and pushed away the strong work ethic of 70's prog. The \\"kickin'\\" drum loops are depressingly boring. Progressive rock has never been about loops or soundscapes, except maybe Krautrock or experimental stuff (like Richard Pinhas). But this isn't Krautrock or pioneering electronic artistry. This is the kind of music that you'd hear at Starbucks or something... <br /> <br />That said, it's not a terrible album on it's own. But when you compare it to the first four Marillion studio albums, it's not worth listening to. <br /> <br />If you want an album to relax to, Marbles (or any Enya album, for that matter) may be your cup of tea. This is not progressive rock. It's something to listen to in bed if you have insomnia...

0307276902|2.0|I read this book when it was first published, primarily because I knew the author had been to treatment at Hazelden. I went through inpatient treatment at Hazelden in 1996 and will have ten years of sobriety this May. Perhaps I was curious to compare his experience to mine--thought he might mention somebody I'd met there, been on the same unit, maybe just to feel a connection with somebody who'd actually written a book and had it published. Regardless, I read the book and was immediately taken aback by the disparity between Jame's Frey's alledgedly factual account of inpatient treatment and my own experience. Without grinding out all the specific details, many of the events this author describes as taking place during his inpatient stay at Hazelden (with what appears to be the tacit approval of staff) seemed highly unlikely. For example, Hazelden has a rigidly enforced policy of non-fraternization between male and female clients. I am not so naive as to think this policy is not subject to violation, but when a man on my treatment unit began communicating with a woman he had met during detox, he was confronted by his peers, the issue was brought to the attention of the unit counselors, and he was discharged from treatment when he continued to pursue the relationship. At least that's how it worked when I was there. And the bit about the dental work. Maybe that's how they performed oral surgery two hundred years ago but let's get real. Nerve blocks with local anesthetics, nitrous, non-narcotic analgesics--there are plenty of ways to provide a patient with pain management without having to give an addict opioids. <br /> <br />I was surprised when I learned the book had been selected for Oprah's Book Club, as I  assumed (incorrectly) that a book of this nature would have undergone some form of editorial vetting process to verify its authenticity prior to being recommended to millions of readers. While Mr. Frey's depiction of the peer-driven therapeautic milieu incorporated into Hazelden's treatment model resonated with my own experience, there were too many inconsistencies which led me to doubt the veracity of the author's story. Obviously, subsequent events have born out those suspicions. <br /> <br />But what disturbed me more than Mr. Frey's taking liberty with the facts (and apparently creating others from whole cloth) was the overall tenor of his nascent recovery. His resistent attitude towards his treatment goals and what has become known as the \\"Minnesota Model\\" of recovery (which incorporates the 12-steps of AA) permeates the book. His actions at the end of the book (sticking his nose in a tumbler of whiskey to demonstrate his victory over the \\"Fury\\") are indicative of a man determined to \\"do it on his own\\" and ignore every scintilla of advice received from his counselors and peers during treatment. When I left treatment I was advised to abstain from all mood-altering substances, go to AA, and stay away from slippery places and slippery people. I did what I was told and so far it's working (and I pray it works again today). The last thing I would have done was put my sobriety to the test by staring down a drink. <br /> <br />I'm not trying to beat the drum for AA. I realize there are plenty of folks who find recovery through other means (I am currently employed as an addiction counselor). But AA is what has worked for me and, in my experience, it is the most successful recovery program out there. Bill W. says in the Big Book \\"the alcoholic [or addict] is an extreme example of self-will run riot\\" and Mr. Frey seems to embody that definition. A core tenet of AA is \\"rigorous honesty\\"--\\"Those who do not recover are people who cannot or will not completely give themselves to this simple program, usually men and woman who are constitutionally incapable of being honest with themselves.\\" Mr. Frey appears to have some work left to do in this area also.  <br /> <br />I hope readers of this book will be captivated by the author's raw,visceral portrayal of the horror of addiction and the benefits to be derived from sharing those experiences with others suffering from the same disease. However, I hope they do not let his story serve as an illustration of an addict moving into a healthy recovery. From a therapist's perspective, much of this story is like a \\"How Not To\\" book of recovery. There are a lot of good books out there dealing with addiction (\\"Drinking: A Love Story\\" by Caroline Knapp; Cool Water: Alcoholism, Mindfulness, and Ordinary Recovery by William Alexander; or, God forbid, the \\"big book\\" of Alcoholics Anonymous--these are just a taste). Don't let this book be your sole exposure to treatment and recovery. And stop in at an open meeting of AA to meet and listen to some \\"real life\\" miracles!

The Blue Mask is the last Lou Reed album with a large collection of the hard edged, riff driven rock songs. This is combined with incredibly personal lyrics that are confessional, shocking, and provocative. <br /> <br />The title cut is a sonic triumph focused on sadomasochistic themes, \\"Take the blue mask down from my face and stick me in the eye. The sinister \\"The Gun\\" brings us into the mind of a gun toting criminal: \\"The man has a gun and he knows how to use it. 6mm Browning, let's see what he can do.\\" The song goes on to tell us just what the man is up to. <br /> <br />Now if all these sound morbid and challenging, well, they are. But the Blue Mask is much more. These songs are juxtaposed with compositions that express incredible passion and empathy. My favorite of these is \\"The Day John Kennedy Died,\\" which showcases Lou as a highly reflective and socially engaged songwriter. <br /> <br />The Blue Mask is certainly a challenging and thought provoking album that is not easily forgotten once heard. If you want to go beyond the popular Lou Reed songs and his most recent more accessible work, this album is for you. Be prepared and enjoy!

0679422714|1.0|The book is garbage of the worst type.  No serious attempt is made to study demographic data in a way which would support the outlandish assertions here.  A more relevant book for someone to read would be Judith Banister's CHINA'S CHANGING POPULATION.  Banister's book was the first serious demographic study of China and it remains an essential must for anyone interested in wading through the dry facts.  A point of comparison that is essential here has to do with the question of the 1960 famine, which this book assigns way over the top numbers to.  Banister herself was also a bit careless since in the text she casually attributes 30 million deaths to this famine.  What becomes clear when you actually look at the year-by-year population and mortality estimates is that there's a big issue at hand here in our choice of standard for the expected normal mortality rates.  Mortality in China dropped by more than 50% between the years 1949 and 1957.  There was a slight rise in mortality for the year 1958, over 1957 but still well under 1949.  Another slight rise in 1959, and then a real famine in 1960 with the mortality dropping back down again in 1961 to just slightly above the 1957 level.  Now of course you're going to compute very different famine death tolls depending upon whether you use 1949 or 1957 as the base year.  I computed a famine death count of 4.35 million by the 1949 standard and of 25.4 million by the 1957 base.  Banister was herself careless in rounding the 25.4 up to 30 without telling the reader that she was specifically using 1957 as her base standard.  But at least Banister provided the actual data for the reader to review and study.  This book does no such thing but just throws out stories which are worth about as much as an Iraqi WMD set to go off on 45 minutes notice.  By the way, a famine with 4.35 million dead against the 1949 standard of expected mortality was nothing unusual in the old China.  See Walter Mallory's classic of 1926, CHINA: LAND OFFAMINE if you wish to know more about the real background of famine in China.  The demographic reality is that mortality rates in China improved in an amazing way because of the revolution and have only suffered some relapses since 1978 as a result of the restoration of capitalism.

B000BNI90O|5.0|I'm a musician, which means I'm pretty good at separating what is good and what is kitch, in terms of music. I've just finished listening to the DVD-Audio version of this soundtrack, and I am absolutely blown away. Firstly, for all the learned researchers out there who continue to muse about whether or not the human ear can actually hear the difference between the enhanced DVD-audio sound and CD-quality sound, I would like to say that yes, there is a difference, and yes, it can be heard. It can be heard in the same way that a good listener can hear the difference between the warmth of a vinyl album and the early sound of the original analogue-to-digital transfers that went into the first commercial CD's. I would ask you to do the following, if you are skeptical: place the DVD-audio version of this soundtrack in your computer (which is the only thing I have a 5.1 set of speakers attached to) and crank up the volume. If the hair on the back of your neck isn't on end after you've made it through the first few minutes of prologue, then you are dead. <br /> <br />As for the music itself, I've never bought a soundtrack album in my life, previous to this. Most soundtracks are crap. This one is absolutely remarkable, even for the slight flaws that have crept into the production. Firstly, I don't actually agree all that much with the one reviewer who finds Ian McKellan singing near the beginning to be disconcerting. I DID find the sudden break between two pretty ominous passages, in order to hear hobbits singing, slightly jarring, but of course this IS a soundtrack following the movie narration, and it DOES actually make sense to create a small break between two heavy sections with a lighter passage. Secondly, there are spots when the DVD moves from one track to another where the audio \\"dies\\" and then returns as the music moves from track to track. And yeah, that's annoying after one has gotten immersed in the music and suddenly is confronted with the reality that this really is a facsimile of a musical performance, and not the performance itself. Otherwise, though, the sound quality here is extraordinary. I've heard the terms \\"pompous\\" and \\"bombastic\\" used to describe Shore's music, but this isn't really fair. The movies themselves are really just a modern interpretation of the Nibelungenlied and a few other Northern European sagas mixed in for good measure, so it makes perfect sense for the soundtrack to have a Wagnerian atmosphere to it. Well, I highly recommend this soundtrack.

B000001G5H|4.0|Pollini took pains as a young artist to mature to his fullest before giving concerts, and this 1972 recital of Chopin Etudes is a leap ahead of his debut disc on EMI. He was always perfecting a combination of edge-of-the-seat virtuosity, musical integrity, and modernist revision of Romantic style that has stood him well for forty years. However, despite the alluring technique and aristocratic bearing, his early pianism wasn't fully fledged yet. Too much of the music on his debut CD is played for surface values without searching deeper. By 1975, when he came to record the 24 Preludes, however, Pollini had grown considerably, and even now his artistry seems to gain in depth year by year--his recent concerts in Carnegie Hall have been miraculous.<br /><br />I am not a piano fancier, so it satisfies me to own this one recording of the Etudes and a few others. Even so, the performance is badly hampered by glassy, harsh sound. One can't listen at loud volume thanks to the brittle edge imparted to the piano. With remastering, Pollini's musicianship would certainly acquire another dimension of power and beauty. I wonder if DG did a remastering for their complete Pollini Edition?<br /><br />P.S. 2013 - For anyone who wants to hear the Etudes recorded in better sound, Testament has released Pollini's first recording, made in Sept. 1960 (over ten days of sessions!) when the pianist was a blazing newcomer of eighteen. He was demanding and fussy, and eventually he disallowed the release of the recording. Now it has resurfaced and displays all the artistry one would expect. The later reading is more polished and articulate in the pristine way of mature Pollini, but I'm grateful to be able to hear him in better sonic surroundings.

B000CNESU8|5.0|Paddy Chayefsky is one of those writers that are relevant in any time (he died at the age of 58), and what could be more relevant in this era than his 1976 film NETWORK (directed by Sidney Lumet)? Just as Stanley Kubrick hung out the absurdities of the Cold War for all to see in DR. STRANGELOVE (OR: HOW I LEARNED TO STOP WORRYING AND LOVE THE BOMB), a film that only becomes more and more potent as time goes on (especially now), so Chayefsky and Lumet attack with full force the TV news industry and its \\"bastardization\\" via corporate control, entertainment masquerading as news, and ratings. The centerpiece of this movie is Howard Beale (the late, great Peter Finch) and his psychotic ramblings that are cynically exploited for the network's gain. And it's also about the people in the network that care only about ratings, especially Diana Christensen (brilliantly played by Faye Dunaway, a truly great actress), programming executive, and Frank Hackett (Robert Duvall, in one of his greatest roles), the new head of the network. Also involved in the madness is Max Schumacher (the truly great actor William Holden), head of the news division and best friend of Beale that ends up having an affair with Diana, and Arthur Jensen (Ned Beatty, who is incredible), the CEO of the corporation that owns the network. <br /> <br />Watching NETWORK 30 years later, with the way television is going now in mind, it is even more astounding to watch the absurdities of the story unfold. Howard Beale's psychotic ramblings about the state of television and the world have a certain herrange-like quality, especially the infamous \\"I'M AS MAD AS HELL, AND I'M NOT GOING TO TAKE THIS ANYMORE!\\" speech! Equally memorable is Jensen's speech about corporations and businesses ruling the world, with Beatty's fire-and-brimstone delivery  (\\"YOU HAVE MEDDLED WITH THE PRIMAL FORCES OF NATURE MR. BEALE, AND I WON'T HAVE IT!\\") lending weight to the absurdity of the story. And in the advent of reality television, the story arc with Laureen Hobbs and her left-wing militia getting their own show is especially funny, especially when they bicker about distribution costs and overhead. <br /> <br />In a time when television is becoming a joke unto itself, NETWORK will never lose its satirical bite. In fact, like DR. STRANGELOVE, Chayefsky's take on network news and television in general will grow more and more potent over the years. And it has one of the greatest punchlines I've ever heard. I consider NETWORK one of the finest movies ever made.

I think after listening to the album a bunch of times, I can try to place these songs into three categories.  The first category consist of the exceptional songs on the album, which are as follows:     1. Intro/Tonight, Tonight -- pretentious perhaps, but I just find it so wonderfully dramatic and full of hope.  A perfect introduction to what follows.    2. 0385503954|5.0|I have not read such a good, magically absorbing novel in a long time! The plot is constructed beautifully, without a flaw. The first part, which is also the longest, describes only one day - and during this day the reader is given the detailed portraits of all the characters (practically enough to feel like any of them), the author builds the tension up to the climax (at the end of the first part), and very believably describes the surroundings and the time.<br /><br />That day in 1935, when there is a lingering shadow of the war, but at the same time its perspective is so remote that all the other aspects of normal life seem to go on normally. The Tallis family in the English countryside mansion prepares for dinner celebrating the visit of the son, Leon. The story in this part is told from the point of view of the youngest sister, thirteen-year old Briony, who has an overgrown imagination and wants to become a writer. To honor her brother, she prepared a play, but some events, invisible perhaps to the others (like jealousy for her two years older and feminine cousin, Lola), lead her to abandon the project of its staging. At the same time, we are witnesses to the love emerging between the older sister, Cecilia, and the son of the resident charlady, Robbie, both freshly back from college. The development of their awareness of the feelings to each other and mutual desire, only in this single day, is exceptionally caught. Briony's fragmentary observations of the couple and her extensive use of the imagination lead to the tragedy, which changes the lives of Robbie and Cecilia forever and lead Briony (very slowly though) into deeper and deeper feeling of guilt.<br /><br />McEwan pushes the reader gently to the right direction, without too much leading and showing the obvious, but it is easy, reading carefully, notice all that he wants to show, ever so subtly. The language is rich, but crystal clear, without any excessive ornamentation, nothing unnecessary, all the words have their use either in the plot, or in the construction of the mood. The next parts create a perfect counterbalance, everything calms down, gets from the complete chaos to the natural order, more static situation - the circumstances are, for a change, very dramatic (a middle of the war actions, a hospital).<br /><br />Already the story, the moral problem and its presentation, are a great start and enough for praise. But there is more, since there is a lot of metatext, comments about literature and the author's role (mostly put into Briony's mouth, but also in the editorial letter from \\"The Horizon\\"), and the ending makes a twist, when old Briony thinks about her story, considering different possible endings. This makes the reader realize that what they just passed through, is just fiction, the writer, its creator, is like God in the fictional world and we are at the end not any closer to the truth than we were at the beginning.<br /><br />I could praise McEwan into infinity here, so as the last sentence I will just say that another of his achievements as a writer is that each of his works is truly original and unique, there is no self-plagiarism in any sense, no repetitive themes, only the ease with which he uses English and his ingenuity remain the same. Five stars don't do him justice.words have their use either in the plot, or in the construction of the mood.
